    \nEpisode I: A Crack In Everything (Part 10)

    They stumbled out into the sand. Charleston banged the door shut behind them. Jimmy jackknifed over and wheezed. "I'm too old for this."

    "Cigarette?" said Charleston.

    Candace and Temperance walked forward. They found a set of obelisks half buried in the sand.

    "King in Yellow," muttered Temperance. "That was an obscure European play in the late nineteenth century. An American named Robert Chambers wrote some short stories about it."

    "Really?" said Candace. "You should be able to find the play at a good used bookstore. I think Penguin came out with a good new scholarly edition a few years ago. 1947, I think...I mean, it's not a very good play."

    "This could have gone a lot worse," said Charleston. "On the plus side, we fulfilled our obligations."

    He and Jimmy stumbled up to the monoliths. Some had toppled over. Beyond them Jimmy could just make out the outlines of a depression or basin, perhaps an ornamental pool of some kind; it had long ago filled with sand and dirt.

    Written on the monoliths were verses of a poem, a single different line on each of the monoliths:

    Expectant we raise our muzzles to smell the air for hatred
    We strain our ears for the sound of love
    We the mute, the lame, the stupid, the dull, the weak
    We turn our blind eyes to the Hunter's killer
    We raise our hands in voices and prayer for an answer
    Nine teeth jut up in the maw of the living Earth:
    Return, Hastur! Heed us!



    "Do you think that Mr. Jennings had anything to do with this?" said Temperance as they trudged back toward the gate to the complex.

    "No, he's just a patsy," said Jimmy.

    "Doesn't mean he won't shoot us," said Charleston.

    "You two have a lot of experience with this," continued Temperance. "How do we save the man?"

    "Percy? Trust me, Percy's gotten everything Percy wanted," said Charleston. "That's how this works. Percy doesn't need to be saved. He's happy with the outcome. Everybody who wasn't happy with the outcome? Those are the guys with their brains blown out."

    "This is true," said Sam. "Why would you want to leave?"

    "That's who Percy was," said Jimmy darkly.

    "Why did you want to stay?" Temperance asked Sam.

    "There's knowledge there!"

    "There's knowledge everywhere," said Charleston. "Pick somewhere else. We can put you back in...but you have to stay down there with him."

    "It's okay, there's other ways to get there."

    "Is there any way to save him?" persisted Temperance. "Or save his soul?"

    "Kill him?" said Jimmy.

    "I'm not sure I understand the question," said Charleston. "World's a big place. Not everyone wants to read the Good Book and lead a good life."



    As they came through the gate they could see Mr. Jennings standing next to two men in dark suits. Jimmy thought immediately that they had the look of government agents. Jennings looked up and pointed at them. His face was petrified with fear.

    One of the agents grabbed him and threw him against the side of the trailer, so hard that he left a deep concave dent in the wall. Jennings' body slumped to the ground and blood pooled around him.

    "Run," said Jimmy, gritting his teeth. He and the rest stumbled over to the car and hastily piled into it.

    Temperance looked back and muffled a shriek. The "agents" weren't casting shadows. Jimmy cranked the engine and slammed down the accelerator.

    "They don't cast shadows," said Temperance quietly. She looked behind them as the car bounded down the dirt road. One of the men in dark suits was jogging behind them, easily pacing them. And even though Jimmy had the engine revving against the redline, she could hear their pursuer say--quite calmly, and even at this distance:

    "He comes."

    But when Jimmy looked back in the rearview mirror there was nobody there...
    \n

    \nEpisode I: A Crack In Everything (Part 11)

    They reached the Gas N' Go and skidded to a halt. Clare Medford came out of the office.

    "How y'all doing? You find what you were looking for?" he said jauntily.

    "We sure did," said Charleston with a toothy grin.

    "I didn't expect to see you back. So soon," Clare hastily added.

    "We found what we were looking for," said Temperance in a silent, terrified voice.

    "Y'all have a nice time, ya hear. Enjoy your trip."



    They drove in silence back to Las Vegas. They rode the elevator up to their suite. Lisa came out to meet them.

    "Oh my God," she said, looking at their faces. She silently poured everyone a drink.

    "I don't drink," said Temperance, tossing off her Scotch.

    "Did you...find anything?" said Lisa anxiously. "It's Percy, isn't it? He's dead?"

    "No," said Sam brightly.

    Jimmy spluttered.

    "He's alive?" said Lisa. "We have to go get him--"

    "He's in a better place," said Sam.

    "I don't understand."

    "He's...the Percy you know is dead," said Jimmy.

    "Is he in any danger?"

    "No...but whoever goes near him will be."

    "Is there...any other danger?"

    "What do you know about the King in Yellow?" said Sam.

    "I've never heard of him," said Lisa.

    "He's a literary figure," said Candace.

    "No he isn't..." muttered Jimmy.



    Later that night Jimmy called the desk for a movie projector. "No problem, chief," said the desk clerk. "You want a live show too? Just know you can't charge admission; don't wanna get in dutch with the vice squad."

    "Just get me the bloody projector," sighed Jimmy.

    The reel of film he had recovered was about fifteen minutes long and quite confusing. Candace immediately recognized that it was obviously based on The King in Yellow, and probably Poe's "Masque of the Red Death"; the plot was similar. But the set design was extremely unusual and rather Expressionistic.

    It was enough, however, for Temperance to track down some details on the film. It was produced by someone named Talbot Estus, who seemed to be dead, and directed by a New York artist named Amadou Lalin--the same person who had painted the picture in Percy's office.

    Meanwhile Candace went through the items they had plundered from Percy's office. Lisa recognized one of the men in the torn up picture.

    "That's Sol," she said. "Solomon Molinari. He was a noted mathematician. Italian. He was Jewish so he fled to England in the late 30s. We used to have tea with him."

    Armed with that, Candace was able to find out that he was currently in a "mental rest facility" in Georgia called Joy Grove. Jimmy thought he recognized the name from somewhere; it turned out that Molinari had been a mountaineer in his youth and part of an Italian expedition that illegally entered Nepal in 1929...in fact, Molinari was the only survivor of the expedition.

    [Outdoorsman use by JP.]

    I was thinking of doing the writeups as ebpubs/mobi for my players as the Obsidian Portal site is a pain to navigate for this stuff, and since they're so bloody long the ebook is a more manageable way to read.

    \n

    Episode II: Angels, Demons and Saints (Part 1)

    \n

    \xa0

    \n

    Oh tangle of matter and ghost

    \n

    Oh darling of angels, demons and saints

    \n

    And the whole broken-hearted host\u2026

    \n

    --Leonard Cohen, \u201cThe Window\u201d

    \n

    \xa0

    \n

    \xa0

    \n

    "Jimmy?" said Candace.

    \n

    Jimmy Wright heaved a huge sigh. "Now?"

    \n

    "You really should."

    \n

    He sighed again, then picked up the hotel telephone. "I need to place a call to Bradley Grey. Yes, the Governor. Yes, I have the number."

    \n

    \xa0

    \n

    Everyone huddled around the phone in Jimmy's room. "Jimmy!" said a rich baritone voice from the phone.

    \n

    "Mr. Grey."

    \n

    "That's Governor Grey to you. What do you have?"

    \n

    "Not much."

    \n

    There was a pause. "Then why the hell are you calling me?" shouted the Governor, loud enough to make Temperance wince.

    \n

    "In my line of duty, not much means something different."

    \n

    Through the static of the long distance lines Jimmy could hear a tinkling like a couple of ice cubes falling into a glass, followed by the glug of something being poured over the ice. It sounded like a very stiff drink.

    \n

    Jimmy sighed once more. "There seems to be a lot of occult involvement."

    \n

    "Again with the occult."

    \n

    "I agree. We found Dr. Schicksal."

    \n

    "He's alive?"

    \n

    "He's...doing well for himself. He's content. I don't think anyone should go look for him."

    \n

    "You're trying to disconcert me. What does Lisa want? Is she gonna follow up?"

    \n

    Jimmy looked at Lisa. "I think we have to get to the bottom of this...the lost time, the mind-control..." she said.

    \n

    "It's gonna be one of those things, Governor," said Jimmy.

    \n

    "Marie! Get me the Jimmy account," said Grey to someone. "What do you need?"

    \n

    "We're going to a place called the Joy Grove Mental Rest Facility in Savannah."

    \n

    Grey sighed. "All right, I'll appoint you as a temporary mental health inspector for the state of California. Good luck. Don't call for a while."

    \n

    \xa0

    \n

    "Ah, Candace, how lovely to hear your voice again. I am trying to teach\xa0Anna Karenin\xa0to moronic freshmen."

    \n

    [Nabokov preferred that version of the name, since most translations don't follow Russian declination for last names. Except that one.]

    \n

    "If anyone can, it would be you."

    \n

    "Have you found anything for your book?"

    \n

    "It's been...fascinating. I think I'm going to stay. We've come across some strange things...I could write volumes of novels."

    \n

    "I advise you as an artist to continue to make time to get this all down."

    \n

    "I'm going to take a sabbatical, I think. I...don't know what I would do without you, Vlady."

    \n

    \xa0

    \n

    "Temperance, is this going to be a thing we do for a while? With Jimmy?" asked Van Carlyle.

    \n

    "Yes, Van. With Jimmy."

    \n

    "So when I was a kid my parents always kept things on the hush\u2014lotta secrets in the Grey household. But I was an adventurous little tyke, and I broke into my dad's files. There was a\xa0lot\xa0of stuff on Jimmy. I think we are gonna need to go incognito a little bit. An excuse to travel around that people won't question. Let me lay it on you, if you're hip."

    \n

    Temperance tried to figure out what that meant. "Sure."

    \n

    "I was thinking of putting together a little trio and going around the country, like a tour."

    \n

    "A trio of\u2014"

    \n

    "A Bop, trio, man, me on keys, a guy on bass, another on skins..."

    \n

    "Are you saying you want to go around the country as a band..."

    \n

    "Yeah."

    \n

    "...with Jimmy Wright? And the novelist?"

    \n

    "I figure that she could say she's writing an article, and Jimmy and Charleston are helping with the band. And you can be in the band. Can you sing?"

    \n

    "Charleston is going to be a...what's the word? Roadie? He's blind."

    \n

    "He can run the board. But the thing is we need some kind of large vehicle. You know anybody who could get one?"

    \n

    Temperance frowned. "Give me a moment to make a call."

    \n

    \xa0

    \n

    "Darling, so good to hear from you!\u201d said Colonel Murphy. \u201cIn the middle of making a big sale. Hollywood! They say they need food trucks! Could be a good market for us!"

    \n

    Temperance held the phone away from her head as the Colonel continued in his usual speaking voice\u2014he hadn't needed to learn how to shout when he'd been in the Marines; he always talked loud enough to be a drill sergeant.\xa0

    \n

    "That's wonderful, pops. I was wondering...well, you wouldn't happen to have any more of your deluxe prototypes? There are a couple of people in the Grey family."

    \n

    "Are you still travelling with Jimmy?"

    \n

    "No. Nooo. No."

    \n

    "Temperance..."

    \n

    "Mr. Wright may be around."

    \n

    "I'll be right out."

    \n

    And a day later an enormous camper pulled into the parking lot of their hotel with Murphy at the wheel. He proudly showed off the impressively detailed interior.

    \n

    "I'm so proud my little girl finally asked me for her adventuring car," sniffed Murphy. "See, here's the dinette. Table folds up for space. Galley kitchen. In the back is a bedroom. And if you close the door to this cabinet, tap twice, and open it up again, it reveals the gun rack."

    \n

    "Gun rack..."

    \n

    "I took ideas from a lot of different places. Maybe you didn't know this, but Jimmy used to have an armored limousine. I'm not gonna say this thing will stop a tank shell. But it should stop small arms fire. We took the engine out of a Kraut tank, and then I had my Germans work on it to make it work with good ol' American parts."

    \n

    [HP: Diesel, I assume.

    \n

    TP: This thing is gonna have its own personal trail of pollution.

    \n

    CP: We're rolling coal!]

    \n

    "I don't know what to say," said Temperance. "Thank you? But is this necessary?"

    \n

    "Well, pumpkin, I always hoped you'd get into the family adventure line. So I had my boys design this a while ago. I was hoping to give it to you when you got married."

    \n

    "I clearly need to talk to Mr. Wright. Why is there garlic and holy water in the gun rack?"

    \n

    "Just saying you can never be too safe."

    \n

    \xa0

    The Unanswered Question (a Tatters of the King remix) [Trail of Cthulhu]

    \n
    \xab12345\xbb

    Comments

      \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nAviatrix \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJanuary 2017 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \nEpisode II: Angels, Demons and Saints (Part 2)\n

      As their DC-4 loped across the long miles from Chicago down to Savannah, Jimmy petted Betty\u2014he'd bought her a seat and charged it to the Governor\u2014and reached deep into one of his coat pockets. He brought out a crumpled, water-stained note. He gingerly unfolded it and read it again:

      \n
      \n

      Dear Boss,

      \nI know you're gonna be real busy today and we won't have time to talk, so I'll just put this in your pocket in case something happens. I just want you to know that you're the best man I've ever met, and it's been an honor.
      Betty Auckland
      \n

      He put the letter back in his pocket. It was a very, very valuable thing; the only tangible reminder he had of another timeline, one where he\u2019d had an employee named Betty, a sweet young thing from Manhattan, Kansas who\u2019d talked like she was from New York, had been a hellion with a shotgun, and lived only to impress him enough to become a \u201creal\u201d private eye. But all that had been wiped away...18 years ago, now. He pushed away the memories. Sometimes he wondered what had happened to Betty in\xa0this\xa0timeline, the one where she had never met him. But he knew that it was better not to find out.

      \n

      He'd done enough damage to time, and vice versa, already.

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      They stepped down from the plane onto the tarmac. Once again Jimmy felt the wet, moist heat slap him in the face. Sweat began pouring down his back, and he mopped his face with a handkerchief. They caught a taxi and went to the only hotel in town he knew, the same one he'd stayed in back in 1937.

      \n

      "How long y'all gonna be stayin'," drawled the front desk clerk.

      \n

      "Couple of days," said Jimmy.

      \n

      "All right then. I can get a taxi if you'n need to get around. Is that you dog? We don't usually allow...dogs."

      \n

      "She's extremely well behaved."

      \n

      "I don't know..."\xa0

      \n

      Jimmy simpered and seemed to shrink. "She's my only companion. These youngins wandering around..." He coughed piteously.

      \n

      "All right, all right."

      \n

      Betty howled as Jimmy led her upstairs.

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      "This is Dr. Keaton," said a man with a thick Southern accent over the phone. "I'm told you're a mental health inspector from California, Mr. Wright? What is it you want from us?"

      \n

      "We want to see how other facilities handle their patients. A sampling."

      \n

      "Ah, you heard about our program for government employees. I'll put you and your people in touch with Dr. Montague. We have a number of interesting patients...well, you'll see when you get here."

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      That evening Candace went down to a college bar. She had hoped to hear some Bop, but she was disappointed. The music was some kind of R&B...but with a stepped up beat.\xa0

      \n

      "What's all this about 'rocking around a clock'?" she asked a cleancut young man.

      \n

      "Latest thing, ma'am. You a professor?"

      \n

      "Not here, but I teach literature. And I'm a writer."

      \n

      "What did you write? I have to read a lot of books, 'cause I'm in college."

      \n

      "It was called\xa0Homefront. It's about a woman and her struggles during the war."

      \n

      "That's heavy. Too bad you ain't a mystery writer."

      \n

      Candace tried not to wince. "Why is that?"

      \n

      It turned out they had an interesting mystery. About a year ago one of the African-American nurses up at the "Mental Rest Facility" had been brutally murdered, almost cut into pieces. But the frightening thing was that there was far too much blood, enough blood for two murders, although that was never made public; nor was any other body ever found. The murder had been blamed on an African-American inmate of the facility.

      \n

      "What's the name of the woman?" asked Candace.

      \n

      "Miss Yates. Fellow they said did it was named Luke."

      \n

      "Thanks so much. Can you tell me where the library is?"

      \n

      He did, and he tried to pick her up, but she made it back to the hotel without any complications.

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      "Dr. Keaton, I'm Mr. Wright. This is my assistant, Miss Heartbrooke." Jimmy was happy that the office was air conditioned; he hadn't worn a suit in years and it was making him sweat even worse.

      \n

      "Charmed, I'm sure," said Dr. Keaton, the Chairman of the Joy Grove Mental Rest Facility. He was balding and wore bifocals. Jimmy vaguely remembered meeting him the last time he had been in Savannah. "I'm afraid I don't know who your other two companions are."

      \n

      "Oh, Mr. Chiu? He's a potential patient, and that's his nurse. Don't worry, he's fine. We want to see how he reacts."

      \n

      "I see," said Keaton. He sighed and shrugged his shoulders. "Well, I suppose that's fine. I'm at the age when I'll be the Chairman Emeritus soon enough, let these young doctors take over. They do have some strange ideas though."

      \n

      He led them on a tour of the hospital; he was especially proud of the new wing, and the air conditioning throughout the facility. "I'm glad you came, I'm sure it's because of our special patients program. One of the Federal administrations sends us their special cases time to time, and they are very generous with their grants. Oh, here's Dr. Montague now."

      \n

      [Incidentally we're now into the actual parts of\xa0Tatters of the King, more or less.

      \n

      A fortyish woman in a lab coat came walking up. She had very red hair and a pair of glasses on a cord around her neck. "Pleased ta' meet you. Edwina Montague," she said with a lilting Scottish brogue.

      \n

      [Well, maybe Edwina had one; unfortunately my concentration on learning Northern Irish accents have degraded my Scottish accent quite a bit.]

      \n

      "Dr. Montague is on a fellowship here. I have her on some of our more difficult cases."

      \n

      "James," said Jimmy, extending his hand.\xa0

      \n

      "Good Scottish name."

      \n

      "I'm not from there."

      \n

      "More's the pity. Is there anyone in particular you wished to see?"

      \n

      "We've heard about Dr. Molinari."

      \n

      "Of course. And," she said conspiratorially, nodding at Charleston, "what may I ask is the situation with him?"

      \n

      "Oh him? He's a potential patient."

      \n

      "Ah, I'll have some of our interns work him up then."

      \n

      "It's a very challenging case," said Jimmy, breaking into a sweat. "Be careful. Bring his handler."

      \nEpisode II: Angels, Demons and Saints (Part 3)\n

      Candace surreptitiously ran through the notes Temperance had compiled on Dr. Molinari. It hadn't been easy to get some of them\u2014they only existed as stories in Italian newspapers....

      \n

      Back in 1929 several professors at the University of Milan had mounted an expedition to Tibet. It was led by Roberto Anzalone and his student Carlo Scipione, along with their colleague Dr. Solomon Molinari. There was also an Italian ex-seminarian named Laurencio Pancetti, an English ex-pat, Malcolm Quarrie, and Roberto del Negro, an Italian soldier from the Alpine division in nominal command.\xa0

      \n

      However, rather than go to Tibet, they illegally entered Nepal. On the night of November 6, 1929, while scaling the heights near a place called Drakmar\u2014an interesting geological site with religious significance\u2014del Negro fell to his death. In the spirit of mountaineering they pressed on, but a blizzard forced them to take shelter in an ice cave, where everyone except Molinari died of exposure. He was found wandering around half-mad by local Buddhist monks.

      \n

      [1-point Library Use by TP spend to get this data. And that really is the spirit of mountaineering!]

      \n

      Dr. Montague led them into the New Wing of Joy Grove, which was decorated in the most modern style. Dr. Molinari's room was at the end of a hall. As they entered, Candace cast her eyes around the rom. It was well-furnished, as befitted a self-committed patient. Most of the walls were covered by bookcases\u2014predominantly mathematical texts, along with picture books on mountaineering. A framed poster celebrated the Italian conquest of K2 in 1954. In the blank spaces between bookcases it seemed Molinari had written equations, interspersed with stylized, elongated drawings of animals. Everything else was neat and austere.

      \n

      Solomon Molinari was seated behind a small table he was using as a desk. He was tall even hunched over in his chair; he had rather wild grey hair, thick horn-rimmed glasses, and a small yarmulke on his head. He looked up rather crossly as they filed in. "Yes, yes, what, who are you, why are you here to see me?"

      \n

      "Although I'm from California, we do inspections of other states to see if we can learn anything, or offer advice," said Jimmy.

      \n

      "Unannounced? I left Italy in 1938 to escape things like this."

      \n

      "You left Italy to come\xa0here?"

      \n

      "No, I left Italy to come to England, then the United States. Now I am here."

      \n

      "That\u2019s a lot of countries.\u201d

      \n

      "I'm European."

      \n

      "It\u2019s pretty standard, Dr. Wright," said Candace.

      \n

      "I am watched over\u2014cared for, I mean; the doctors are nice, especially Dr. Edie," said Molinari.

      \n

      "I think Dr. Wright would like to know what you were working on that caused you to come here," said Candace.

      \n

      "I was working on a government project. The stress was a little too much for me."

      \n

      "I'll be on my way to see about your Chinese friend," said Dr. Montague.\xa0

      \n

      "Chinese, Italians, what else do we need here," muttered Molinari. "What do you want to know? I'll tell you what I can. Some is still classified."

      \n

      "Why don't we start with that," said Jimmy.

      \n

      "I was working on a project called TAROT. It was an interesting project, headed by a friend of mine, Percival Schicksal."

      \n

      "Is he still alive?"

      \n

      "I don't know. The details of my last days are somewhat hazy in my recollection ...Percival was a very interesting man, artistically talented...he brought many different thinkers, artists together. Of course, much of it was nonsense, like the name of the project. We all had a Tarot card assigned to us."

      \n

      "Oh really?" said Candace. "Which were you?"

      \n

      "The Magician. Card number one."

      \n

      "Why?"

      \n

      "An interesting question. In the Tarot of Marseille, the card is a mountebank, a con artist. But in more recent versions of the deck, he is a real magician, in the sense Crowley meant. Percival was The Fool, which is apparently a card of great significance."

      \n

      Jimmy stared. "I have some other questions, I'm just not sure how to phrase them. You see, we've been to the facility."

      \n

      "I was not under the impression there was a lot there anymore."

      \n

      "There wasn't...except for the trap door held down by a heavy oil tank."

      \n

      "Ah. You've been to the labs. Are you going to ask me about mind control? Because we weren't doing that. Not really. We had chemicals that had interesting effects on the psyche. We investigated them, and methods of propaganda...some from the last century..."

      \n

      "Why exactly did Dr. Schicksal choose you?"

      \n

      Molinari leaned back and sighed. "When I was younger, I was a member of a secret society\u2014a social club, see? It was called\xa0I fratelli del segno giallo. You don't speak Italian. The brotherhood of the yellow sign. And I suppose you know I was on an expedition...we were all members, except for del Negro; the government wouldn't let us leave without a fascist."

      \n

      "Can I ask you about the trip?"

      \n

      "It was terrible. Everyone died except me. How I survived, I do not know."

      \n

      "And why did you go to Nepal instead of Tibet?"

      \n

      "The Brotherhood...mostly were Christian mystics. But they had a degree of syncretism. It is why I was allowed to join. They were interested in the Buddhist mystics of that part of Nepal."

      \n

      [Here I asked for an Interpersonal spend to get more from the good doctor, as he had kept his silence for thirty years about the expedition.]

      \n

      "Dr. Molinari, let's be honest here," said Candace. "I know this must be difficult, that it was hard to come to this place, even if it was for your health. We're not here to cause you any trouble...but we honestly need to know what is happening, what happened at that location."

      \n

      "You're a very sweet young woman...you remind me of Juliet. Ah, I shouldn't talk about them, they're probably all dead. Except for Carl, and he writes the most ridiculous things." He gestured at a mimeographed newsletter on his desk.

      \n

      "I think talking to us could be an important step in dealing with these things."

      \n

      [Reassurance spend by CP.]

      \n

      "You are like Juliet, if only she had stayed with Carl, maybe he wouldn't be so crazy. It's too dangerous to tell you everything. We always meant to go to Nepal. Tibet was a feint. We went to Drakmar...to get in touch with the Divine."

      \n

      "That's a noble goal," said Candace.

      \n

      "That's never a good idea," said Jimmy.

      \n

      "I can't say I disagree with either of you. In the final moment...Scipione went insane and killed del Negro. And Anzalone killed Quarrie but died in the struggle. Pancetti was already lost at that point. By the grace of the Sign I returned...how I do not know. I don't know why I was spared."

      \n

      "The Sign?" said Candace.

      \n

      "The Yellow Sign. What we were brothers of. It is a rune of mystic importance...but you should not seek it out. It is not healthy...but much like my friend Amadou, I sometimes wonder if I made the right decision."

      \n

      "Which decision?"

      \n

      "To stop Quarrie."

      \n

      "Yes, you did," said Jimmy.

      \n

      \nEpisode II: Angels, Demons and Saints (Part 4)\n

      Dr. Montague chatted with Temperance and Charleston as she took them to an examination room. She had trained as a surgeon, but during the London Blitz came to the conclusion that psychological wounds were more difficult to treat than physical ones and changed her profession.

      \n

      "What's the name of your friend," she asked.

      \n

      "Mr. Chiu."

      \n

      "And his first name?"

      \n

      "Charleston," said Charleston.

      \n

      "You're kidding me."

      \n

      "I am the original. I predate the candy bar. I feel like they owe me a check."

      \n

      "We've talked about this, Charleston," said Temperance.

      \n

      In response to her question, Charleston told Dr. Montague he had lost his sight in a terrible accident, showing her the nasty scar across his forehead. "It happened when I was much, much younger, still a teenager."

      \n

      "You don't think about hurting yourself?"

      \n

      "I don't think I understand the question. People hurt themselves all the time."

      \n

      "He seems to have megalomaniacal tendencies," put in Temperance. "Talking himself up at inappropriate moments. Delusions of grandeur. Sometimes goes wandering where he ought not be. Problems with social norms."

      \n

      "Most of our patients here are non-violent, save for Mr. Harriwell, who butchered that nurse. But had we turned him over to the authorities, he'd have gone to the gas chamber. The poor man lacks his wits; so we arranged to keep him here."

      \n

      Violent cases at the Rest Facility, Edie elaborated, were quite rare, although because it was a secure environment, they had a number of FBI and other government employees who had "seen a little too much."

      \n

      "Nobody's accused me of seeing too much," said Charleston.

      \n

      "What an interesting...p'raps sense of humor is a stretch," said Edie.

      \n

      "He falls back on gallows humor," said Temperance. "We're hoping that you can cure the puns and the quips."

      \n

      "I dinna allow I'm such a good psychiatrist. I could cut his tongue out clean."

      \n

      "I hear you can do wonders with electroshock."

      \n

      "Aye, a marvelous new therapy. Verra interesting results. Still calibrating, still calibrating...an excellent way to lift depression."

      \n

      "Did you say lift\xa0oppression?" said Charleston.

      \n

      "Depression. D'ye nae speak English?"

      \n

      She led them into an interview room where two interns were licking their chops over diagnosing Charleston.

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      "Tell me more about Amadou," said Candace.

      \n

      "A questing soul, a gentle soul. Very, very close to Percival," said Dr. Molinari. "Very close."

      \n

      "Ah," said Jimmy.

      \n

      "Sono un uomo del mundo. Percival brought him in because of his inspiration, an area we should mine in the human psyche. Turns out he was right, he was useful...although the child was a little broken. I heard he is in San Francisco, or New York, where he studied...I'm not sure, he left before me. I think at the end he felt Percivale had gone a little too far."

      \n

      "You said you stopped Malcolm Quarrie from doing something, back in 1929," said Candace. "What was it?"

      \n

      "From...from bringing a god down to Earth."

      \n

      "I'm so glad you stopped him," said Jimmy.

      \n

      "I...am not sure if it was the right thing. They called him...il re en giallo. The King in Yellow. Roberto killed Quarrie to prevent His coming. When I returned, I was in no condition to look for Pancetti...I told everyone that he died. He must have died. None of us should have survived."

      \n

      "Tell me what you remember about your last days at TAROT," said Jimmy.

      \n

      "Percival...hoped to speak to a god."

      \n

      "Why do people always want to speak to a god?"

      \n

      "Why\xa0wouldn't\xa0you want to speak to one? Do you never think of this, to know the answers to the universal questions?"

      \n

      "It's never worth knowing."

      \n

      "I have to disagree with you."

      \n

      "Do you see the chaos you cause\u2014"

      \n

      "Do you see the chaos outside? Let me tell you. This was June 1953. On June 16, when we began our tests, there were riots and strikes in East Germany. All throughout the world, there was a tremor of freedom, of breaking of restraint. While we were doing the working! With that, that Cassie!"

      \n

      "Cassie?"

      \n

      "Some girl Percival found. He said she was magic. The world was changing! And don't think this was the first time this ever happened: 1848. 1794. All these times. 1903, the Boxer Rebellion. Talk to Carl, he'll tell\u2014no. I say too much."

      \n

      "The gods don't give a damn about us," said Jimmy.

      \n

      "You know this for sure? You've talked to God?"

      \n

      "My share of them. They do horrible, horrible things."

      \n

      "Forgive Dr. Wright," said Candace. "He gets worked up."

      \n

      "I understand. You I see have a true questing nature. Think about it, what we could learn!"

      \n

      "Insanity," said Jimmy.

      \n

      "How do you know this is sanity? This is an ancient belief in the world. Study the Greeks, study Dionysus instead of being a useless civil servant! Now get out of my room!"

      \n

      "We could have gotten more information out of him," said Candace when they were back in the hall, "if you hadn't talked nonsense!"

      \n

      "Gods! Insanity!"

      \n

      "Who are you to judge? The man is obviously ill. Maybe\xa0you\xa0should be in here."

      \n

      "Maybe you're right."

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      \nEpisode II: Angels, Demons and Saints (Part 5)\n

      The interns ran Charleston through a variety of psychological tests of greater and greater depth. They were interested in his accident.

      \n

      "I find it traumatic to think about it," said Charleston.

      \n

      "Look mac, you're in a mental hospital," said one intern. "It's like having a heart attack in a hospital, it's the best place to do it."

      \n

      "I saw a flash of metal and then darkness."

      \n

      "Someone knifed you in the eyes? and scooped them out?"

      \n

      "It was a lucky hit, just not for me. I feel it was a turning point in my life. There are things you're better off not seeing."

      \n

      "I don't know. You've never seen Jean Harlow or Marilyn Monroe."

      \n

      "You're right. I guess I'd just have to feel it out."

      \n

      The interns proceeded to describe the ladies in graphic detail. They offered to shoot Charleston up with lithium. "Have your nurse check in with us, we're very interested in your case."

      \n

      "I've had a violent past."

      \n

      "How violent? You have trauma?"

      \n

      Charleston agreed he might. He asked about Luke Harriwell. Nobody had figured out how he had gotten out of his cell, the interns said, explaining that Harriwell had been a Tuskegee airman who'd gotten into a car accident after the war. Brain damage was so extensive that he now had a child's mind.

      \n

      "And he dismantled a nurse?" said Charleston incredulously.

      \n

      "Maybe he had a flashback to the war. What we don't know is how he got out. Maybe he had a key."

      \n

      "You don't always need a key to get out of a locked room."

      \n

      "Wait, what?"

      \n

      "I'm just trying to be honest. Back when I was in my twenties I had trouble facing my troubles, so I had to learn to get out of rooms I didn't want to be in."

      \n

      "What are you, a contortionist?"

      \n

      [Me: in that you twisted space and time around...]

      \n

      "I contorted some things, you could say."

      \n

      "I gotta say, mac, you don't sound crazy."

      \n

      "It's been twenty years," said Charleston wolfishly. "I've had a lot of practice."

      \n

      "I like this guy. We should keep him around just for laughs."

      \n

      "Maybe I could share a room with Luke."

      \n

      "Hell no! You might kill each other! We don't want that, we're on a strict no killings policy! And we can't keep him in restraints all the time. That's inhuman."

      \n

      "You do keep him in them sometimes?"

      \n

      "Sure, when we take him out of the cell...hey, we should put them together."

      \n

      "Yeah," said the other intern. "We might get a paper out of it."

      \n

      A while later they led Luke, a large African-American man, into the room. They kept heavy shackles on his wrists, and chained him to the table in front of Charleston. "I don't know no Chinese man," said Luke.

      \n

      "Neither do I, I'm born and raised in Shanghai. Shanghai West Virginia."

      \n

      "I don't know no one from West Virginia either. Can't shake hands."

      \n

      "I can't see them to shake anyway."

      \n

      "Okay."

      \n

      Charleston relaxed and gently felt out with his seventh sense. He could tell that whatever Luke had seen, it was something more than a human terror.

      \n

      [CP and I worked this out as a way for Charleston to sometimes overcome his blindness; he has a ridiculous amount of Cthulhu Mythos as justification.]

      \n

      "I saw the Devil," said Luke. He lisped a bit:\xa0debbil.

      \n

      "I've spoken to the Devil," whispered Charleston.

      \n

      "You can't be from the Devil, you still got skin. The Devil ain't got no skin. I saw him cut up that sister lady."

      \n

      [CP: Does being skinless ring a bell?

      \n

      Me: Yeah, there are various skinless entities, but you can't tell which.

      \n

      CP: Skinless entity that dismembers doesn't narrow it down?

      \n

      Me: Pretty much, yeah.

      \n

      CP: The company I keep.]

      \n

      "Why did the Devil chop her up and leave you alone?"

      \n

      "I don't know. Maybe the Devil wanted a lady. Maybe he come back for me."

      \n

      "Did the woman say anything to the Devil?"

      \n

      "Mostly she just scream. The Devil said things. He got a high-pitched voice. I was mostly screaming. I seen him walking outside my room. I were in it. The Devil opened up my room, that's why they think I did that thing. Sister weren't in my room, they don't let the ladies come to my room at night. That's a pity. I used to have a wife. She was nice to me."

      \n

      "So the Devil...walked past your door, slaughtered this poor nurse..."

      \n

      "I suppose you could say he was in the process of slaughtering. Then he open my door and yelled at me. 'You get out there boy, or I come kill you too.' So I run out, then they catch me. He had blue eyes. Blue-eyed devil."

      \n

      [TP: Wait a minute...

      \n

      JP: (laughs)

      \n

      Me: And she sticks the landing!]

      \n

      "What about Miss Yates, who died?"

      \n

      "She were nice to me. She came in to feed me. Say, good morning Luke."

      \n

      "What kind of knife did the Devil have?"

      \n

      "Soldier knife, like what I had in war."

      \n

      "That's how I lost these," said Charleston, as Temperance helped Luke draw a sketch of something that resembled a bayonet.

      \n

      "I gotta say, you don't seem crazy to me," continued Charleston.

      \n

      "That what I say, but they say Luke, you can't take care of yourself no more. And they probably right. I ain't seen the skinless Devil no more."

      \n

      "Kinda cowardly."

      \n

      "I ain't talking bout no Devil, he come back for me."

      \n

      "Was he bleeding?" asked Temperance.

      \n

      "Oh yes."

      \n

      "Did you see if there was a trail..."

      \n

      "The thing I seen that was very strange is that he cut a big slice of skin offa Miss Yates, and then cut the same slice of skin off himself. And he kept doing like that. 'Cept the blood stopped and his skin start grow back."

      \n

      "Well, that's devilish."

      \n

      "It's black magic. Like them Gullah folk do."

      \n

      "You talk to any doctors about this?" asked Charleston.

      \n

      "Yup. All of them. They say, Luke you gotta talk about this. Mostly they is the male doctors like talking to you\u2014the ones there, laughing at you. And sometimes Miss Edie, she nice to me."

      \n

      "You ever see anyone else?" said Temperance.

      \n

      "Sometimes, when they let me walk in the yard. They all government people."

      \n

      "Veterans?"

      \n

      "I suppose. Some of them real messed up. Worse'n Luke."

      \n

      "You're all right."

      \n

      "Okay. Thank you, you a nice lady."

      \n

      "You fear God, Luke?" said \xa0Charleston.

      \n

      "Oh yes. I fear Him. And I fear the Devil."

      \n

      "Which one you pray to?"

      \n

      "To God! Ridiculous question, Luke praying to the Devil. You a mean old man."

      \n

      "Maybe that's why the Devil spared you."

      \n

      "Maybe. Power of Jesus came down over me. I'd read the Bible if I could, but I can't read no more, since the accident."

      \nEpisode II: Angels, Demons and Saints (Part 6)\n

      They reconvened back at the hotel and discussed the strange case of Luke Harriwell. Candace had pulled a bunch of newspaper records from the library. Much to her chagrin she found out that Luke's wife Martha had died a year ago of a heart attack. Nobody had told him.

      \n

      Jimmy took the time to drop by the local chief of police. He let a little of his old camaraderie with the police leak into his personality. The police seemed to feel that Luke should have been sent to the gas chamber...or taken care of, if you know what I mean. Fortunately for Luke, the Mental Rest Facility had good security.

      \n

      "Did he have any blood on him?"

      \n

      "No, he must have cleaned up. But he had the knife. Military knife. We sent that up to the Georgia Bureau of Investigation. We do things proper here, despite what they may tell you in Cal-eee-forn-ya. Gotcha yourselves too many freaks and dropouts..."

      \n

      After listening to the chief\u2019s unprompted racist tirade, Jimmy was able to browbeat the chief into turning over Luke's file to him. He was quite surprised by some of the details\u2014the cuts were very precise, especially considering that Miss Yates had been running...at first. To his non-medical eye this smacked of surgical precision, not anything like what Luke would have been capable of.

      \n

      [Cop Talk spend by JP to get the file.]

      \n

      He spent the next couple of days working on trying to identify the knife, even to the point of swallowing his pride and calling some of the people he used to know in the FBI.

      \n

      "Jimmy? My god, I heard you died in Peoria."

      \n

      "That's the story I'm getting. I have a knife that I need identified. Lemme send the picture up to you."

      \n

      The next day his friend called back. "It's a British military knife, mostly issued to the Home Army during the Blitz."

      \n

      [Research spend by JP.

      \n

      There was some discussion of the facts of the case:

      \n

      HP: Did they think he hid the knife in his room? Was the door forced? Was there a key missing?

      \n

      Me: Hey, they did a perfectly acceptable job of framing this up!]

      \n

      The next day there was another murder.

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      The papers of course had a field day splashing Luke's picture all over the front page. Another nurse, Miss Long, had been butchered in much the same fashion as poor Miss Yates. Undoubtedly there would have been enormous pressure to drag him out of the hospital...if anyone knew where he was. He had vanished.

      \n

      Jimmy drove them back up to the hospital. Dr. Keaton was quite agitated but relieved to see friendly faces. He didn't know how Luke had escaped; he wondered if he had had help.

      \n

      "And I have to take on some cases now," he groused. "Dr. Montague isn't here right now. You know how it is, it's very hard for a psychiatrist to get the help they need...well, anyway, she took a leave of absence, heading out to the West Coast somewhere. A conference of some sort. And she was so distraught this morning! She had a real rapport with Luke. And she really is a caring woman. She seems frosty on the exterior but that's just her being Scottish. The warm climate here has mellowed out her blood a bit though."

      \n

      Temperance bit her lip and tried to remember that Dr. Keaton had been trained in the 1920s.

      \n

      The sad details came out. The police thought that the knife used had been a scalpel, not any of the regular knives\u2014all those were tightly controlled since the last incident.

      \n

      "Looks like our killer has a particular predilection for the lovely ladies," said Charleston.

      \n

      "Very often serial murder is a sexual crime," said Dr. Keaton. "The killer murders out of some deep seated sexual dysfunction. It's not a crime of passion."

      \n

      Jimmy asked if they could be shown around. "I'm not sure how this comes under your\xa0regulatory\xa0purview," muttered Keaton, but Temperance pointed out how a glowing report on how they dealt with the situation would go over well in Washington.

      \n

      [Bureaucracy spend by TP.]

      \n

      Luke's room showed no sign of being forced. He hadn't been kept in restraints, so if somebody had opened it he could just walk out. Temperance decided to ask Dr. Keaton if there was anywhere that Luke could hide himself that might have been missed on the initial sweeps.

      \n

      "Well, there is the old maximum security wing," said Dr. Keaton. "We don't use it anymore. There was an experimental program run there in the 1930s that didn't work out."

      \n

      Jimmy resolutely looked away.

      \n

      [Indeed, it was the location of a scene in Eternal Lies.]

      \n

      "Why don't you tear things like that down?" said Charleston.

      \n

      "Contractors, permits, it's a nightmare to do that kind of work."

      \n

      With some grousing, Dr. Keaton agreed to take them to the old wing, cautioning them that the standards of care in those days were not up to the level of today. And so it proved; many of the cells had rotting padding on the walls, and slots on the bottom of their doors to slide food into them.

      \n

      Candace was the one who noticed a larger than average amount of flies buzzing in and out of a closet.\xa0

      \n

      "Did the police search this area?" she asked Dr. Keaton.

      \n

      "They said they did."

      \n

      "Well, no reason to doubt their efficiency," said Charleston. "Let's move on." He and Jimmy pushed forward, leaving Candace behind. She gulped nervously, and then opened the door.

      \n

      Luke Harriwell had been stuffed in there. His throat had been slit, and his eyes removed. Post-mortem, Candace thought, as she screamed.\xa0

      \n

      "What was that?" said Dr. Keaton from the next ward.

      \n

      "Sounds like a woman screaming," said Charleston.

      \n

      "Of course it does! This is as bad as that day in 1937. I never did get to the bottom of that. And my best assistant quit! I wonder what happened to Bethany Mae..."

      \n

      Temperance ran back to Candace. "What's going on," she began, and then saw Luke's body.

      \n

      She started to scream as well and they began to pray in Latin.

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      Of course the cops came. They hadn't missed the body in their previous sweep; whoever had killed Luke had moved his body. Charleston thought that the removal of the eyes was psychologically significant: as if he had seen something he wasn't supposed to have seen.

      \n

      [Criminology and Psychology.]

      \n

      "His throat was cut surgically," said Jimmy.

      \n

      "Yeaaaah," said Temperance. "And where did Edie go?"

      \n

      "Out west. And she started at Joy Grove about a year ago...right before Miss Yates died."

      \nEpisode II: Angels, Demons and Saints (Part 7)\n

      They took the next flight out to Los Angeles, winging through St. Louis and Denver. The RV was waiting for them, along with Lisa Schicksal, who was eager to talk to them. Jimmy and Candace ran over the names Dr. Molinari had mentioned: Juliet, Carl, and Amadou. Lisa knew Carl Simon a bit, but not the others.

      \n

      Temperance decided to start tracking down the actors from the reel of film Jimmy had recovered in Nevada. Although she wasn't a filmmaker, it was evident that the film was made in a largish studio. She decided to track down an acquaintance of her mother, the actress Olivia Clarendon.

      \n

      [Photography or Accounting would have put them on the direct trail to the studio and who hired it, but nobody had those abilities. So TP spent High Society to talk to Olivia, who is a thinly veiled Olivia de Haviland. (She was a character in Eternal Lies.)]

      \n

      "You must be Temperance," said Olivia when they met at her room in the Beverly Hills Hotel. It overlooked the swimming pool where a couple of bleached blonde sun worshippers languished in a way they hoped would attract attention. "I knew your mother slightly, through Mr. Wright. Shame that he died in Pakistan." She had a mid-Atlantic accent, a bit more polished than Cary Grant's.

      \n

      "There are so many rumors about his death."

      \n

      "Did he not die? I'd dearly like to see him again. Such an amusing little man. You had a question about a film?\xa0The Unanswered Question? Doesn't wring a bell."

      \n

      Temperance had brought the reel, and they watched it together in silence. "Well, that was a piece of shite," said Olivia. "I know who must have shot it. Talbot Estus. Has his lighting style all over it. He's not bad, just one of those avant-garde types."

      \n

      "Art for art's sake?"

      \n

      "Oh yes. I met him at a party once. Unfortunately he's dead. Killed himself on New Year's Day, 1954. I suppose I could have my man reach out to his agent and see what he was working on."

      \n

      A few days later Temperance got a mimeographed sheet of paper from one of the lower-tier talent agencies in the city. It had the cast list of\xa0The Unanswered Question:

      \n

      Michael Gillen (The Stranger, costumes)

      \nJean Hewart (Camilla)
      George Keith (The Prince, nobles)
      Hannah Keith (Cassilda)
      Walter Paige (Noble, The King)
      Cinematography by Talbot Estus
      Written and directed by Amadou Lalin
      \n

      A little bit of research turned up some details: Michael Gillen had mostly been a costumer (he had worked with Estus on all his prior films), not an actor; he owned a small clothing boutique in Hollywood. Jean Hewart had been trained as an opera singer and returned to that career; she was a singer in the San Francisco Opera Company. George and Hannah Keith still worked in Hollywood, George as a studio executive and Hannah as a character actress.

      \n

      Walter Paige wasn't in Hollywood at all; he worked for an insurance company in New York. A quick call to him by Temperance turned up that he was mostly embarrassed by his brief college fling with acting, especially the movie in question.

      \n

      Temperance rang up George and Hannah Keith and arranged for the entire crew to have lunch with them. George didn't have much to say; he'd played the Prince and some background nobles. He described the shoot as a bit crazy and said that while Amadou had a number of very artistic ideas about the sets and lighting (and had written the dialogue), most of the actual directing had been done by Talbot Estus, who had experience with blocking and the physical details of making a movie.

      \n

      Hannah was more thoughtful. "It was a ridiculous thing. As far as I know it was never distributed or even shown. Amadou was a kind of ethereal guy. The thing is, I don't think I ever did any better work. Although I had really terrible nightmares for months after we wrapped."

      \n

      She had worked with Talbot on previous projects. She didn't know any of the actors, although she had grown close to Jean who played her daughter (at least, her daughter sometimes) Camilla. Jean had been excited to do the movie; she had mostly done stage work but wanted, like so many young actresses, to break into movies. She'd enjoyed her time very much.

      \n

      "I'm a perfectly \xa0adequate actress. I know I'll never be Bette Davis," said Hannah.

      \n

      "Don't sell yourself short, kid," said Charleston.

      \n

      "Acting is my job. I show up and do it, really well. But Jean...she's a real artist, an opera singer; the work really affected her. I can give you her number if you want to speak to her."

      \n

      One curious thing, Hannah said, was that almost all the special effects had been done "in camera", that is without using any post-production trickery. She still wasn't sure how some of them had been captured\u2014like the sequence where the King replaced her without anyone noticing in Act II. They'd filmed it at two in the morning on a drafty sound stage in Culver City.

      \n

      "Did you keep any mementos? Props?" asked Charleston.

      \n

      "No, when we struck Amadou made us give everything back. A bunch of guys in dark suits kicked us all out and drove him off...I guess to Nevada, I think that's where he lived."

      \n

      Before they ended lunch, Hannah showed them pictures of her infant daughter. Her name was Camille.

      \n

      [TP: Oooh, creepy child.

      \n

      CP: (in character???) All children are creepy.

      \n

      HP: Why are all children\xa0creepy?

      \n

      CP: I don't know, ask them.

      \n

      Me: Take back a point of Stability...both of you.]

      \nEpisode II: Angels, Demons and Saints (Part 8)\n

      Michael Gillen was, understandably, nervous around talking to them; Jimmy was finally able to convince him they weren't cops on a vice bust. His years on the road had made him wise to gay subculture.

      \n

      [Counterculture 2+ got the foot in the door; the group has a paucity of Reassurance, we discovered.]

      \n

      "Strange shoot. Talbot made some films, they didn't go over so well," Michael told them over coffee. They were sitting in his design studio in the back room of his shop; dressmakers dummies leered into the corners, and falls of silk and satin draped from racks. It was getting near awards season, after all.

      \n

      Michael was a pleasant man in his forties, with greying curly hair and a soft Texas accent. "People called them pornographic," he continued. "Ridiculous, they just didn't understand the European art Talbot was influenced by. Can't do male nudity in America. Poor Talbot had nightmares that kept getting worse and worse after we made that picture. I tried to do what I could for him...he told me he regretted making the film, that he never should have done it. But also that he would never make anything that good again."

      \n

      "What were the nightmares about?" asked Jimmy.

      \n

      Wandering in a strange city, said Michael. Sometimes being on the sets, but they had somehow become real. And sometimes just being chased. Talbot had had spells where he would wake up and still be asleep but couldn't move; he said that when it happened he would see dark figures with red eyes who suffocated him. Michael had had a dream like that as well once. Never so scared in my life, he told them, and that he didn't see how anyone could last long having those dreams every day.

      \n

      "I didn't like my part. It was small but important. I didn't like it...I had to wear a mask all the time." He'd kept it; it was a pure white mask made to look like porcelain, with the features just black slits. It resembled the painting Amadou had made for Percival.

      \n

      [CP was interested if anything was ceremonial or mystic about the mask; I offered a Cthulhu Mythos spend...]

      \n

      Charleston didn't like the mask. He knew something about the mythos of Hastur and the King in Yellow, although like most people he was unclear about what the difference between them was. The main thing he knew was that there was always repetition in the stories: there were always the two Ladies, and the King, and the Stranger.

      \n

      And sometimes the Stranger chased down people who had seen the Yellow Sign.

      \n

      "I don't know where Amadou is, and I don't care. If he hadn't hired Talbot, he'd still be alive today," concluded Michael sadly.

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      Before they left for San Francisco, Candace called up Nabokov. "We have quite the mystery going on...and I saw something a few days ago that I have never seen before...it terrified me."

      \n

      "Would you like to talk about it?" said Vladimir in his cultured voice.

      \n

      "It was terrible. A man who was accused of murder was murdered."

      \n

      "Ah, how Dostoevskian. I hate Dostoevsky."

      \n

      [Actually true! I have Nabokov's lecture notes from when he taught at Cornell.]

      \n

      "I understand," said Nabokov, "what a terrible shock this must have been for you. I left Russia because of the Revolution. My father was assassinated. But I have found, that although these were terrible experiences, and difficult to talk about, they have engaged my writing. As an artist you must plumb the depths of your own terror. Certainly the book I have just completed forced me to dig deep into my own psyche and learn things about myself that were perhaps not good things to learn. But as a true artist, you must pursue your vision. Unless of course you're just going to use it as material for those ridiculous things you churn out."

      \n

      [The book was, of course,\xa0Lolita.]

      \n

      "I'm not going to...deny that some of these ideas would be interesting..."

      \n

      "Oh, Candace, one day you will make a commitment to your art. I understand. I have to go teach these morons about\xa0The Idiot. Take care."

      \n

      Candace hung up. She was still shaken, and couldn't help but wonder if what her husband had seen in the war wasn't like what she had seen in Savannah.

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      They drove up the coast in Temperance's enormous RV, swaying along the Pacific Coast Highway and down the peninsula until the misty hills of San Francisco came into sight. The harbor was full of cargo ships heading to the wharves; there was also a Navy destroyer coming back to port. Temperance remembered her father working there during the war; Jimmy had shipped through there on missions for the OSS as well.

      \n

      He wondered about the private eye he'd hired in the city to look into Amadou; he wasn't returning his calls.

      \n

      Candace was excited; she wanted to visit City Lights Bookstore, Lawrence Ferlinghetti's shop where he published exciting new poets (including himself) and books banned in the US.

      \n

      Charleston wondered if he'd run into Gary Snyder, a local nature poet who had tracked him down a few times asking questions about mystical aspects of Buddhism. Charleston had been annoyed, but swapped stories about some of his more esoteric beliefs. Snyder had mentioned some of his friends, Kerouac and that guy...Gainsborough? Ginsberg. He'd heard they'd moved out to the coast from New York in the last year.

      \n

      \nEpisode II: Angels, Demons and Saints (Part 9)\n

      Jean Hewart turned out to be singing Zerlina in the San Francisco Opera's production of\xa0Don Giovanni. Temperance called up the box office and, not without trepidation, dropped her mother's name. As a great patroness of the arts, Mrs. Cuevas had a lot of pull; the manager was\xa0happy\xa0to agree to let Miss Murphy come backstage to talk to the singers. He gave her a box seat for the next performance, on October 6.

      \n

      [Did my research when picking the opera. I tried to find an important but minor role that would suit an up-and-coming young singer...and okay, I'd just seen\xa0Don Giovanni\xa0again at the Met.

      \n

      This was a High Society spend by TP.]

      \n

      Jean Hewart was tall for an actress; underneath her dark wig her hair was dirty blonde and soaked in sweat. She casually waved for Temperance to take a cup of tea when she came to her dressing room; in the meantime she took off the rest of her costume.

      \n

      "You're Ruby's daughter, is that right? Nice to meet you. Yes, I did some acting in the movies, small parts mostly."

      \n

      "I was wondering about one called\xa0The Unanswered Question..."

      \n

      "Oh my god, that piece of crap? I was young, I wanted to be a film actress. I have the looks, but it's hard to break into Hollywood. You either have to be willing to do something, or know someone. And I didn't, so I came back to singing. And it's gone very well! To be honest, I never thought I'd make it into a major company. I mean, I'm good...but I even while in school I never thought I'd end up here."

      \n

      "What was the movie like?"

      \n

      "Oh, it was terrible. Well, actually I don't think I ever watched the whole thing. Talbot and Amadou were both crazy. Maybe they're geniuses...I don't know film. The thing is, though, ever since then I've had this powerful artistic inspiration. I took the year off, toured Europe, met some singing teachers. Saw\xa0a lot\xa0of operas. I sang on the streets of Venice, Paris nightclubs...and when I got back to the States, all my auditions went so well. I don't know if it was Amadou...but it was some kind of creative\xa0unlocking."

      \n

      "Hannah said something similar."

      \n

      "Yes, she called and said you might come. I had some strange dreams myself. Mostly about the King, a still form...but his robes moved without any wind. How did that song Hannah sang go?\xa0Where flap the tatters of the King. Still don't know why they gave\xa0her\xa0the song. Oh, and a great dark city. It was different every time, but I knew it was the same city. And a lake...and the suns setting behind it. It was disturbing. It felt real somehow...almost prophetic. I never felt like bothering Amadou about it. He's a lovely kid\u2014the bent ones always are. I did like him a lot."

      \n

      "Have you seen him since?"

      \n

      "No, but actually a PI named Tuck came by asking about him. So Dashiell Hammett\u2014appropriate, don't you think? He wanted to know if I'd seen Amadou, or some guy named Bacon. Is there something I need to worry about? Is this about money? If there's a residual I want to know."

      \n

      "From what I've heard it's never been released. Just a bit of film history I happened to stumble into."

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      Jimmy went to the address he had for Vince Tuck. It was on a fog-shrouded street down one of \xa0the dark streets of San Francisco. It was also over a Chinese restaurant. He climbed the steps to the office but found a note tacked to it indicating that Tuck had gone fishing for a couple of days.

      \n

      Jimmy sighed and fished out his well-worn lockpicking kit. The door was easy to spring, although he scratched up the lockplate. The room beyond was dirty and chaotic. It smelled of stale sweat and drink. Jimmy poured himself a shot from the bottle on top of several folders piled on the desk. There didn't seem to be anything about his case, although there was a map of San Francisco with some pins in it hanging behind the desk. And there was a safe underneath the desk. Jimmy went to work on it, but he wasn't up on post-war models, and he didn't have the right tools to crack it.

      \n

      He sighed again and let himself out.

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      The next morning Candace and Charleston headed down to Pacific Heights to find City Lights. It proved to be a small triangular store on a street corner. Much to Candace's disappointment, Ferlinghetti wasn't in; his assistant Shig Murao was running the counter.

      \n

      "We don't carry braille," he said to Charleston.

      \n

      "Don't worry about it. I'm with a friend."

      \n

      Shig gestured them towards the new editions of Henry Miller and D.H. Lawrence that had come in. Candace asked about Amadou.

      \n

      "Amadou Lalin? I know that cat," said Shig. "Haven't seen him around much, he's in and out, but he's in town right now. Hey, I've got it\u2014go to Gallery Six tonight. Bunch of local guys, Snyder, that cat Ginsberg from New York, they're all gonna read tonight. Heard that Ginsberg's been working on something big that he's gonna read from."

      \n

      Charleston wanted to know about what was new and hip; Shig told him that most of the "cats" who were doing the really interesting work hadn't been published yet\u2014like Kerouac; he'd read a little bit of some novel he'd written about driving around the country with Neal Cassady that was fantastic.\xa0

      \n

      "I dig," said Charleston. "This guy we're looking for made a movie that was never shown."

      \n

      "Oh yeah, I heard stories about that thing. Supposed to be really weird. You ever hear of the Asylum?"

      \n

      Charleston gulped. "We're new in town."

      \n

      "It's a floating party, a real happening happening. They show that film there sometimes, they say." Shig explained that the location changed all the time; the only way to find out the next place it would be at was to find posters with some weird character written on them. People who went to the Asylum didn't seem to have any trouble finding it, but other people didn't seem to be able to find the posters.\xa0

      \n

      Charleston got Shig to trace the "strange character" on his hand. It resembled the weird mark they'd found in Nevada. Of course.

      \n

      [Charleston and Candace both have Counterculture 2, so they didn't have any trouble getting information from a hepcat like Shig.]

      I do like the Counterculture skill. Must remember to tell Bill White.
      \nYeah, once I got a look at All Along the Watchtower and understood it working like Credit Rating it clicked a lot more and has been useful. I'm not using Credit Rating in this game (we have High Society and Counterculture, for the 50s that probably works) or in my brand new Tuesday Night Meetup run of Masks of Nyarlathotep--there I replaced Credit Rating with the same 4 Social Class skills from the 1890s Dracula Dossier. This wasn't anything against Credit Rating per se, just that my simplified character building didn't have a great way to assign Credit Rating so I went with that instead.

      \nEpisode II: Angels, Demons and Saints (Part 10)\n

      They got back to their hotel rooms to discover that Van Carlyle and his band had been practicing in one of them\u2014Van didn't have a piano to play so he mostly shouted encouragement to the other two members of the trio: Mencius, the bass player, a tall African-American man who always wore sunglasses and a little fur hat, and Bob Bricknell, a skinny white kid on the drums. A pall of blue and fragrant smoke hung near the ceiling.

      \n

      "We rigged up a sheet like a curtain so we'd know what it looked like," said Van to Temperance. "Mencius\u2014lay down that riff again." Charleston and Candace nodded in time; the bassist was really quite good. Van took Temperance out into the hallway.\xa0

      \n

      "Say, do you have any bread so I could rent a piano and have them carry it up here?" he asked.

      \n

      "You're going to give the waitstaff\xa0bread\xa0to do that for you?"

      \n

      "I don't mean\xa0bread\xa0bread, I mean bread."

      \n

      "Cheddar!" put in Charleston.

      \n

      "Exactly. You are hip, daddy!" said Van.

      \n

      "You want...money?" said Temperance. "Didn't your father give you a budget?"

      \n

      "I don't like to bring the Governor in on things. We have kind of a policy where he don't ask anything and I don't tell him nothing."

      \n

      "Don't ask, don't tell," said Charleston. "I could see that catching on."

      \n

      "Whoa. That's good. Hey Mencius! That's the name of our number in the third set! Thanks man, that's gone! You get what I'm laying down."

      \n

      "Friend of mine was a bandleader, sort of. Back before the war. Makes me old thinking about it," said Charleston.

      \n

      "Where you from? New York? Got a weird accent."

      \n

      "I'm a weird guy."

      \n

      "Hey, I punched my ticket on that train. Just wondering if you were up at the Cotton Club and digs like that back then. Art Tatum! That cat taught me everything. I wore the\xa0grooves\xa0out on those records."

      \n

      "Nothing like seeing it live."

      \n

      "True," said Mencius, not missing a beat.

      \n

      "What are they talking about?" asked Temperance.

      \n

      "Music," said Jimmy. He lurched downstairs to arrange a new room for her.

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      Jimmy was getting ready when he saw Lisa sitting in her room, fidgeting with a newspaper. She looked rather sad and lonely. He fingered the engagement ring he kept in his pocket, and then grabbed Temperance.

      \n

      "Let's take her," he said. She shrugged, and went in with him to invite Lisa.

      \n

      "I don't know what to wear to a poetry reading," she said.

      \n

      "I'm sure it's very artistic," said Temperance.

      \n

      \xa0

      \n

      And so it proved. Gallery Six was a little hole in the wall place in Pacific Heights. As soon as they came in, they were swarmed with the music and energy of the room. Someone was pounding out jazz on an upright piano in one corner. Cigarette smoke loomed in dusky clouds through the air. Girls with their hair down, wearing slacks and flats or no shoes at all were dancing to the beat.

      \n

      Temperance looked down at her nice grey skirt suit and tasteful heels. Lisa blinked behind her glasses and unconsciously smoothed down her cocktail dress. "This is literary?"

      \n

      "Oh, yeah," said Candace, bobbing her head to the rhythm.

      \n

      "I should have gone to more poetry readings."

      \n

      "Hey lady, should I take your wrap?" leered some hipster at Temperance.

      \n

      "Thank you, it is very warm. I'll need a coat check."

      \n

      Jimmy shook his head, dropped a fin into the "Support our Artists" basket, and led them to a seat.

      \n

      Gary Snyder did indeed make an appearance, reading a poem about bears and blackberries. People kept coming and going, and celebrating loudly the lines they liked. One man of about thirty was especially loud; he was swigging from a jug of wine, passing it around from time to time while he shouted loud cries of "Yeah!" and "Go, man, go!" up at the stage. Next to him was a handsome man a few years younger who talked a mile a minute.\xa0

      \n


      \n

      A somewhat nebbishy man with thick black-rimmed glasses made his way to the front. He placed a sheaf of papers on the lectern, and then in a thick New York accent said:

      \n


      \n

      "Howl. For Carl Solomon." He bagan to read:

      \n



      \n
      \n

      I saw the best minds of my generation destroyed by madness, starving hysterical naked,

      \ndragging themselves through the negro streets at dawn looking for an angry fix,
      angelheaded hipsters burning for the ancient heavenly connection to the starry dynamo in the machinery of night,
      who poverty and tatters and hollow-eyed and high sat up smoking in the supernatural darkness of cold-water flats floating across the tops of cities contemplating jazz...


      \n

      [Yes, I actually read all that, in the closest approximation to Ginsberg's voice I could manage.]

      \n

      Temperance looked at Lisa, who was staring with rapt fascination bordering on horror. She realized that she must have something of the same look on her face as well. She tried to take it as well as she could and not let on how un...hip? she was, although the line\xa0

      \n

      who howled on their knees in the subway and were dragged off the roof waving genitals and manuscripts,

      \nwho let themselves be fucked in the ass by saintly motorcyclists, and screamed with joy,
      who blew and were blown by those human seraphim, the sailors, caresses of Atlantic and Caribbean love,
      who balled in the morning in the evenings in rosegardens and the grass of public parks and cemeteries scattering their semen freely to whomever come who may...
      \n

      caused her to blink rather hard. One didn't say "genitals" in public, after all.

      \n

      [I'm pretty sure that my group had never encountered\xa0Howl\xa0before. At this point I handed out my little card on the Beats, although they weren't called that yet.]

      \n
